      is (depending on the reference) 2 to 4 parts per million,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciTechEncy_8-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-SciTechEncy-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      or about 40 times as abundant as
      <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">
       silver
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Earth's crust from the surface to 25 km (15 mi) down is calculated to contain 10
      <sup>
       17
      </sup>
      kg (2
      <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
       ×
      </span>
      10
      <sup>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        17
       </span>
      </sup>
      lb) of uranium while the
      <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">
       oceans
      </a>
      may contain 10
      <sup>
       13
      </sup>
      kg (2
      <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
       ×
      </span>
      10
      <sup>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        13
       </span>
      </sup>
      lb).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciTechEncy_8-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-SciTechEncy-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The concentration of uranium in soil ranges from 0.7 to 11 parts per million (up to 15 parts per million in farmland soil due to use of phosphate
      <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">
       fertilizers
      </a>
      ), and its concentration in sea water is 3 parts per billion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Uranium is more plentiful than
      <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">
       antimony
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">
       tin
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">
       cadmium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">
       mercury
      </a>
      , or silver, and it is about as abundant as
      <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">
       arsenic
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">
       molybdenum
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Uranium is found in hundreds of minerals, including uraninite (the most common uranium
      <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">
       ore
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Carnotite" title="Carnotite">
       carnotite
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Autunite" title="Autunite">
       autunite
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Uranophane" title="Uranophane">
       uranophane
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Torbernite" title="Torbernite">
       torbernite
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Coffinite" title="Coffinite">
       coffinite
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Significant concentrations of uranium occur in some substances such as
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">
       phosphate
      </a>
      rock deposits, and minerals such as
      <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">
       lignite
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">
       monazite
      </a>
      sands in uranium-rich ores
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (it is recovered commercially from sources with as little as 0.1% uranium
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ).
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Citrobacter_freundii.jpg">
        <img alt="Five cylinder-like bodies on a flat surface: four in a group and one separate." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="462" data-file-width="700" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Citrobacter_freundii.jpg/220px-Citrobacter_freundii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Citrobacter_freundii.jpg/330px-Citrobacter_freundii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Citrobacter_freundii.jpg/440px-Citrobacter_freundii.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Citrobacter_freundii.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Citrobacter" title="Citrobacter">
          Citrobacter
         </a>
        </i>
        species can have concentrations of uranium in their bodies 300 times the level of the surrounding environment
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Some bacteria, such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Shewanella_putrefaciens" title="Shewanella putrefaciens">
        Shewanella putrefaciens
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Geobacter_metallireducens" title="Geobacter metallireducens">
        Geobacter metallireducens
       </a>
      </i>
      and some strains of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Burkholderia_fungorum" title="Burkholderia fungorum">
        Burkholderia fungorum
       </a>
      </i>
      , use uranium for their growth and convert U(VI) to U(IV).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some organisms, such as the lichen
      <i>
       Trapelia involuta
      </i>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">
       microorganisms
      </a>
      such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterium" title="Bacterium">
       bacterium
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Citrobacter" title="Citrobacter">
        Citrobacter
       </a>
      </i>
      , can absorb concentrations of uranium that are up to 300 times the level of their environment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Citrobacter
      </i>
      species absorb
      <a href="/wiki/Uranyl" title="Uranyl">
       uranyl
      </a>
      ions when given
      <a href="/wiki/Glycerol_phosphate" title="Glycerol phosphate">
       glycerol phosphate
      </a>
      (or other similar organic phosphates). After one day, one gram of bacteria can encrust themselves with nine grams of uranyl phosphate crystals; this creates the possibility that these organisms could be used in
      <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">
       bioremediation
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">
       decontaminate
      </a>
      uranium-polluted water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The proteobacterium
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Geobacter" title="Geobacter">
        Geobacter
       </a>
      </i>
      has also been shown to bioremediate uranium in ground water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonVrionis2003_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AndersonVrionis2003-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The mycorrhizal fungus
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glomus_intraradices" title="Glomus intraradices">
       Glomus intraradices
      </a>
      increases uranium content in the roots of its symbiotic plant.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In nature, uranium(VI) forms highly soluble carbonate complexes at alkaline pH. This leads to an increase in mobility and availability of uranium to groundwater and soil from nuclear wastes which leads to health hazards. However, it is difficult to precipitate uranium as phosphate in the presence of excess carbonate at alkaline pH. A
      <i>
       Sphingomonas
      </i>
      sp. strain BSAR-1 has been found to express a high activity alkaline phosphatase (PhoK) that has been applied for bioprecipitation of uranium as uranyl phosphate species from alkaline solutions. The precipitation ability was enhanced by overexpressing PhoK protein in
      <i>
       E. coli
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">
       Plants
      </a>
      absorb some uranium from soil. Dry weight concentrations of uranium in plants range from 5 to 60 parts per billion, and ash from burnt wood can have concentrations up to 4 parts per million.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dry weight concentrations of uranium in
      <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">
       food
      </a>
      plants are typically lower with one to two micrograms per day ingested through the food people eat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Production_and_mining">
       Production and mining
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">
       Uranium mining
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U_production-demand.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="940" data-file-width="1400" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/U_production-demand.png/220px-U_production-demand.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/U_production-demand.png/330px-U_production-demand.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/U_production-demand.png/440px-U_production-demand.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U_production-demand.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        World uranium production (mines) and demand
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA-WUM_60-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-WNA-WUM-60">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yellowcake.jpg">
        <img alt="A yellow sand-like rhombic mass on black background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="613" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Yellowcake.jpg/220px-Yellowcake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Yellowcake.jpg/330px-Yellowcake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Yellowcake.jpg/440px-Yellowcake.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yellowcake.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Yellowcake" title="Yellowcake">
         Yellowcake
        </a>
        is a concentrated mixture of uranium oxides that is further refined to extract pure uranium
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Worldwide production of U
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      O
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      (yellowcake) in 2013 amounted to 70,015
      <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">
       tonnes
      </a>
      , of which 22,451 t (32%) was mined in
      <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">
       Kazakhstan
      </a>
      . Other important uranium mining countries are
      <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
       Canada
      </a>
      (9,331 t),
      <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
       Australia
      </a>
      (6,350 t),
      <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">
       Niger
      </a>
      (4,518 t),
      <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">
       Namibia
      </a>
      (4,323 t) and
      <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
       Russia
      </a>
      (3,135 t).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA-WUM_60-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-WNA-WUM-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Uranium ore is mined in several ways: by
      <a href="/wiki/Open-pit_mining" title="Open-pit mining">
       open pit
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Underground_mining_(soft_rock)" title="Underground mining (soft rock)">
       underground
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_leach" title="In-situ leach">
       in-situ leaching
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Borehole_mining" title="Borehole mining">
       borehole mining
      </a>
      (see
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">
       uranium mining
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks479_6-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks479-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Low-grade uranium ore mined typically contains 0.01 to 0.25% uranium oxides. Extensive measures must be employed to extract the metal from its ore.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem774_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem774-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      High-grade ores found in
      <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_Basin" title="Athabasca Basin">
       Athabasca Basin
      </a>
      deposits in
      <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">
       Saskatchewan
      </a>
      , Canada can contain up to 23% uranium oxides on average.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Uranium ore is crushed and rendered into a fine powder and then leached with either an
      <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">
       acid
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">
       alkali
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Leachate" title="Leachate">
       leachate
      </a>
      is subjected to one of several sequences of precipitation, solvent extraction, and ion exchange. The resulting mixture, called
      <a href="/wiki/Yellowcake" title="Yellowcake">
       yellowcake
      </a>
      , contains at least 75% uranium oxides U
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      O
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      . Yellowcake is then
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcined" title="Calcined">
       calcined
      </a>
      to remove impurities from the milling process before refining and conversion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Commercial-grade uranium can be produced through the
      <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">
       reduction
      </a>
      of uranium
      <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">
       halides
      </a>
      with
      <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">
       alkali
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">
       alkaline earth metals
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Uranium metal can also be prepared through
      <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">
       electrolysis
      </a>
      of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       KUF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        5
       </span>
      </span>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tetrafluoride" title="Uranium tetrafluoride">
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        UF
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         4
        </span>
       </span>
      </a>
      , dissolved in molten
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium_chloride" title="Calcium chloride">
       calcium chloride
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       Ca
       <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">
        Cl
       </a>
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">
       sodium chloride
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">
       Na
      </a>
      Cl) solution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Very pure uranium is produced through the
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_decomposition" title="Thermal decomposition">
       thermal decomposition
      </a>
      of uranium halides on a hot filament.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Resources_and_reserves">
       Resources and reserves
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      It is estimated that 5.5 million tonnes of uranium exists in ore reserves that are economically viable at US$59 per lb of uranium,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      while 35 million tonnes are classed as mineral resources (reasonable prospects for eventual economic extraction).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAResourcesDemand_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IAEAResourcesDemand-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Prices went from about $10/lb in May 2003 to $138/lb in July 2007. This has caused a big increase in spending on exploration,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_64-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      with US$200 million being spent worldwide in 2005, a 54% increase on the previous year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAResourcesDemand_65-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IAEAResourcesDemand-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This trend continued through 2006, when expenditure on exploration rocketed to over $774 million, an increase of over 250% compared to 2004. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">
       OECD
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Energy_Agency" title="Nuclear Energy Agency">
       Nuclear Energy Agency
      </a>
      said exploration figures for 2007 would likely match those for 2006.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_64-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Australia has 31% of the world's known uranium ore reserves
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the world's largest single uranium deposit, located at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympic_Dam,_South_Australia" title="Olympic Dam, South Australia">
       Olympic Dam
      </a>
      Mine in
      <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">
       South Australia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There is a significant reserve of uranium in
      <a href="/wiki/Bakouma" title="Bakouma">
       Bakouma
      </a>
      a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-prefecture" title="Sub-prefecture">
       sub-prefecture
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Prefecture" title="Prefecture">
       prefecture
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Mbomou" title="Mbomou">
       Mbomou
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">
       Central African Republic
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Some nuclear fuel comes from nuclear weapons being dismantled,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      such as from the
      <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">
       Megatons to Megawatts Program
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      An additional 4.6 billion tonnes of uranium are estimated to be in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_water" title="Sea water">
       sea water
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
       Japanese
      </a>
      scientists in the 1980s showed that extraction of uranium from sea water using
      <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">
       ion exchangers
      </a>
      was technically feasible).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UseaWater_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UseaWater-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordCohen_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-stanfordCohen-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There have been experiments to extract uranium from sea water,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but the yield has been low due to the carbonate present in the water. In 2012,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ORNL" title="ORNL">
       ORNL
      </a>
      researchers announced the successful development of a new absorbent material dubbed HiCap which performs surface retention of solid or gas molecules, atoms or ions and also effectively removes toxic metals from water, according to results verified by researchers at Pacific Northwest National Laboratory.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Supplies">
       Supplies
      </span>
     </h3>

     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MonthlyUraniumSpot.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="641" data-file-width="979" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/MonthlyUraniumSpot.png/220px-MonthlyUraniumSpot.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/MonthlyUraniumSpot.png/330px-MonthlyUraniumSpot.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/MonthlyUraniumSpot.png/440px-MonthlyUraniumSpot.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MonthlyUraniumSpot.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Monthly uranium spot price in US$ per pound. The
        <a href="/wiki/Uranium_bubble_of_2007" title="Uranium bubble of 2007">
         2007 price peak
        </a>
        is clearly visible.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uraniumingo_74-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-uraniumingo-74">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 2005, seventeen countries produced concentrated uranium oxides, with
      <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
       Canada
      </a>
      (27.9% of world production) and
      <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
       Australia
      </a>
      (22.8%) being the largest producers and
      <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">
       Kazakhstan
      </a>
      (10.5%),
      <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
       Russia
      </a>
      (8.0%),
      <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">
       Namibia
      </a>
      (7.5%),
      <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">
       Niger
      </a>
      (7.4%),
      <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">
       Uzbekistan
      </a>
      (5.5%), the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      (2.5%),
      <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
       Argentina
      </a>
      (2.1%),
      <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">
       Ukraine
      </a>
      (1.9%) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">
       China
      </a>
      (1.7%) also producing significant amounts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Kazakhstan continues to increase production and may have become the world's largest producer of uranium by 2009 with an expected production of 12,826 tonnes, compared to Canada with 11,100 t and Australia with 9,430 t.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the late 1960s, UN geologists also discovered major uranium deposits and other rare mineral reserves in
      <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">
       Somalia
      </a>
      . The find was the largest of its kind, with industry experts estimating the deposits at over 25% of the world's then known uranium reserves of 800,000 tons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bufais_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bufais-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The ultimate available supply is believed to be sufficient for at least the next 85 years,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAResourcesDemand_65-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-IAEAResourcesDemand-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      although some studies indicate underinvestment in the late twentieth century may produce supply problems in the 21st century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MITfuelSupply_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MITfuelSupply-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Uranium deposits seem to be log-normal distributed. There is a 300-fold increase in the amount of uranium recoverable for each tenfold decrease in ore grade.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In other words, there is little high grade ore and proportionately much more low grade ore available.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">
       Compounds
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_reactions.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="821" data-file-width="1143" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Uranium_reactions.svg/350px-Uranium_reactions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Uranium_reactions.svg/525px-Uranium_reactions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Uranium_reactions.svg/700px-Uranium_reactions.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_reactions.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Reactions of uranium metal
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Oxidation_states_and_oxides">
       Oxidation states and oxides
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides">
       Oxides
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:333px;max-width:333px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U3O8lattice.jpg">
          <img alt="Ball and stick model of layered crystal structure containing two types of atoms." data-file-height="493" data-file-width="817" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/U3O8lattice.jpg/200px-U3O8lattice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/U3O8lattice.jpg/300px-U3O8lattice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/U3O8lattice.jpg/400px-U3O8lattice.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:127px;max-width:127px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UO2lattice.jpg">
          <img alt="Ball and stick model of cubic-like crystal structure containing two types of atoms." data-file-height="743" data-file-width="821" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/UO2lattice.jpg/125px-UO2lattice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/UO2lattice.jpg/188px-UO2lattice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/UO2lattice.jpg/250px-UO2lattice.jpg 2x" width="125"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="clear:left">
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
        <a href="/wiki/Triuranium_octoxide" title="Triuranium octoxide">
         Triuranium octoxide
        </a>
        (left) and
        <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">
         uranium dioxide
        </a>
        (right) are the two most common uranium oxides.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Calcined uranium yellowcake, as produced in many large mills, contains a distribution of uranium oxidation species in various forms ranging from most oxidized to least oxidized. Particles with short residence times in a calciner will generally be less oxidized than those with long retention times or particles recovered in the stack scrubber. Uranium content is usually referenced to
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        8
       </span>
      </span>
      , which dates to the days of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan_project" title="Manhattan project">
       Manhattan project
      </a>
      when
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        8
       </span>
      </span>
      was used as an analytical chemistry reporting standard.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">
       Phase relationships
      </a>
      in the uranium-oxygen system are complex. The most important oxidation states of uranium are uranium(IV) and uranium(VI), and their two corresponding
      <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">
       oxides
      </a>
      are, respectively,
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">
       uranium dioxide
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_trioxide" title="Uranium trioxide">
       uranium trioxide
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem779_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem779-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_oxide" title="Uranium oxide">
       uranium oxides
      </a>
      such as uranium monoxide (UO), diuranium pentoxide (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        5
       </span>
      </span>
      ), and uranium peroxide (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
       ·2H
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       O
      </span>
      ) also exist.
     </p>
     <p>
      The most common forms of uranium oxide are
      <a href="/wiki/Triuranium_octoxide" title="Triuranium octoxide">
       triuranium octoxide
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        8
       </span>
      </span>
      ) and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANL-Chem_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ANL-Chem-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Both oxide forms are solids that have low solubility in water and are relatively stable over a wide range of environmental conditions. Triuranium octoxide is (depending on conditions) the most stable compound of uranium and is the form most commonly found in nature. Uranium dioxide is the form in which uranium is most commonly used as a nuclear reactor fuel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANL-Chem_82-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ANL-Chem-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At ambient temperatures,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      will gradually convert to
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        8
       </span>
      </span>
      . Because of their stability, uranium oxides are generally considered the preferred chemical form for storage or disposal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANL-Chem_82-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ANL-Chem-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Aqueous_chemistry">
       Aqueous chemistry
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U_Oxstufen.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="450" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/U_Oxstufen.jpg/150px-U_Oxstufen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/U_Oxstufen.jpg/225px-U_Oxstufen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/U_Oxstufen.jpg/300px-U_Oxstufen.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U_Oxstufen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Uranium in its oxidation states III, IV, V, VI
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Salts of many
      <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">
       oxidation states
      </a>
      of uranium are water-
      <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">
       soluble
      </a>
      and may be studied in
      <a href="/wiki/Aqueous_solution" title="Aqueous solution">
       aqueous solutions
      </a>
      . The most common ionic forms are
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        3+
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      (brown-red),
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        4+
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      (green),
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        +
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      (unstable), and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        2+
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      (yellow), for U(III), U(IV), U(V), and U(VI), respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem778_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem778-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A few
      <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">
       solid
      </a>
      and semi-metallic compounds such as UO and US exist for the formal oxidation state uranium(II), but no simple ions are known to exist in solution for that state. Ions of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        3+
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      liberate
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
       water
      </a>
      and are therefore considered to be highly unstable. The
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        2+
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ion represents the uranium(VI) state and is known to form compounds such as
      <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_carbonate" title="Uranyl carbonate">
       uranyl carbonate
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_chloride" title="Uranyl chloride">
       uranyl chloride
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_sulfate" title="Uranyl sulfate">
       uranyl sulfate
      </a>
      .
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        2+
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      also forms
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex (chemistry)">
       complexes
      </a>
      with various
      <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">
       organic
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">
       chelating
      </a>
      agents, the most commonly encountered of which is
      <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_acetate" title="Uranyl acetate">
       uranyl acetate
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem778_83-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem778-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Unlike the uranyl salts of uranium and
      <a href="/wiki/Polyatomic_ion" title="Polyatomic ion">
       polyatomic ion
      </a>
      uranium-oxide cationic forms, the
      <a href="/wiki/Uranate" title="Uranate">
       uranates
      </a>
      , salts containing a polyatomic uranium-oxide anion, are generally not water-soluble.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Carbonates">
       Carbonates
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The interactions of carbonate anions with uranium(VI) cause the
      <a href="/wiki/Pourbaix_diagram" title="Pourbaix diagram">
       Pourbaix diagram
      </a>
      to change greatly when the medium is changed from water to a carbonate containing solution. While the vast majority of carbonates are insoluble in water (students are often taught that all carbonates other than those of alkali metals are insoluble in water), uranium carbonates are often soluble in water. This is because a U(VI) cation is able to bind two terminal oxides and three or more carbonates to form anionic complexes.
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; float:center">
      <caption>
       <a href="/wiki/Pourbaix_diagram" title="Pourbaix diagram">
        Pourbaix diagrams
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medusa_84-0">

         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </caption>
      <tr>
       <td>

           </a>
           <div class="thumbcaption">

      </tr>

        </a>

        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medusa_84-1">

       </td>
