       <td>
        <div class="center">
         <div class="thumb tnone">
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_no_carbonate.png">
            <img alt="A graph of potential vs. pH showing stability regions of various uranium compounds" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="662" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_no_carbonate.png/220px-Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_no_carbonate.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_no_carbonate.png/330px-Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_no_carbonate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_no_carbonate.png/440px-Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_no_carbonate.png 2x" width="220"/>
           </a>
           <div class="thumbcaption">
            <div class="magnify">
             <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_no_carbonate.png" title="Enlarge">
             </a>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td>
        <div class="center">
         <div class="thumb tnone">
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_carbonate_present.png">
            <img alt="A graph of potential vs. pH showing stability regions of various uranium compounds" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="714" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_carbonate_present.png/230px-Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_carbonate_present.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_carbonate_present.png/345px-Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_carbonate_present.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_carbonate_present.png/460px-Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_carbonate_present.png 2x" width="230"/>
           </a>
           <div class="thumbcaption">
            <div class="magnify">
             <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_fraction_diagram_with_carbonate_present.png" title="Enlarge">
             </a>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Relative concentrations of the different chemical forms of uranium in a non-complexing aqueous medium (e.g.
        <a href="/wiki/Perchloric_acid" title="Perchloric acid">
         perchloric acid
        </a>
        /sodium hydroxide).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medusa_84-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-medusa-84">
          [84]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td>
        Relative concentrations of the different chemical forms of uranium in an aqueous carbonate solution.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medusa_84-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-medusa-84">
          [84]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_pH">
       Effects of pH
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The uranium fraction diagrams in the presence of carbonate illustrate this further: when the pH of a uranium(VI) solution increases, the uranium is converted to a hydrated uranium oxide hydroxide and at high pHs it becomes an anionic hydroxide complex.
     </p>
     <p>
      When carbonate is added, uranium is converted to a series of carbonate complexes if the pH is increased. One effect of these reactions is increased solubility of uranium in the pH range 6 to 8, a fact that has a direct bearing on the long term stability of spent uranium dioxide nuclear fuels.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrides.2C_carbides_and_nitrides">
       Hydrides, carbides and nitrides
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Uranium metal heated to 250 to 300 °C (482 to 572 °F) reacts with
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      to form
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hydride" title="Uranium hydride">
       uranium hydride
      </a>
      . Even higher temperatures will reversibly remove the hydrogen. This property makes uranium hydrides convenient starting materials to create reactive uranium powder along with various uranium
      <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">
       carbide
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nitride" title="Nitride">
       nitride
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">
       halide
      </a>
      compounds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Two crystal modifications of uranium hydride exist: an α form that is obtained at low temperatures and a β form that is created when the formation temperature is above 250 °C.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_carbide" title="Uranium carbide">
       Uranium carbides
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_nitride" title="Uranium nitride">
       uranium nitrides
      </a>
      are both relatively
      <a href="/wiki/Inert" title="Inert">
       inert
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Semimetal" title="Semimetal">
       semimetallic
      </a>
      compounds that are minimally soluble in
      <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">
       acids
      </a>
      , react with water, and can ignite in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">
       air
      </a>
      to form
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        8
       </span>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Carbides of uranium include uranium monocarbide (U
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       C
      </a>
      ), uranium dicarbide (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UC
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ), and diuranium tricarbide (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       C
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      ). Both UC and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UC
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      are formed by adding carbon to molten uranium or by exposing the metal to
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">
       carbon monoxide
      </a>
      at high temperatures. Stable below 1800 °C,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       C
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      is prepared by subjecting a heated mixture of UC and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UC
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      to mechanical stress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem780_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem780-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Uranium nitrides obtained by direct exposure of the metal to
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
       nitrogen
      </a>
      include uranium mononitride (UN), uranium dinitride (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UN
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ), and diuranium trinitride (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       N
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem780_86-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem780-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">
       Halides
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg">
        <img alt="Snow-like substance in a sealed glass ampoule." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="683" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg/220px-Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg/330px-Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg/440px-Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">
         Uranium hexafluoride
        </a>
        is the feedstock used to separate uranium-235 from natural uranium
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      All uranium fluorides are created using
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tetrafluoride" title="Uranium tetrafluoride">
       uranium tetrafluoride
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      );
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      itself is prepared by hydrofluorination of uranium dioxide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Reduction of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      with hydrogen at 1000 °C produces uranium trifluoride (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      ). Under the right conditions of temperature and pressure, the reaction of solid
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      with gaseous
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">
       uranium hexafluoride
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        6
       </span>
      </span>
      ) can form the intermediate fluorides of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       F
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        9
       </span>
      </span>
      ,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       U
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
       F
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        17
       </span>
      </span>
      , and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        5
       </span>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At room temperatures,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        6
       </span>
      </span>
      has a high
      <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">
       vapor pressure
      </a>
      , making it useful in the
      <a href="/wiki/Gaseous_diffusion" title="Gaseous diffusion">
       gaseous diffusion
      </a>
      process to separate the rare uranium-235 from the common uranium-238 isotope. This compound can be prepared from uranium dioxide and uranium hydride by the following process:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        UO
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       + 4 HF →
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        UF
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         4
        </span>
       </span>
       + 2
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        H
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
        O
       </span>
       (500 °C, endothermic)
      </dd>
      <dd>
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        UF
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         4
        </span>
       </span>
       +
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        F
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       →
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        UF
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         6
        </span>
       </span>
       (350 °C, endothermic)
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The resulting
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        6
       </span>
      </span>
      , a white solid, is highly
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">
       reactive
      </a>
      (by fluorination), easily
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">
       sublimes
      </a>
      (emitting a vapor that behaves as a nearly
      <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">
       ideal gas
      </a>
      ), and is the most volatile compound of uranium known to exist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One method of preparing
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tetrachloride" title="Uranium tetrachloride">
       uranium tetrachloride
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UCl
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      ) is to directly combine
      <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">
       chlorine
      </a>
      with either uranium metal or uranium hydride. The reduction of
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UCl
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      by hydrogen produces uranium trichloride (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UCl
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      ) while the higher chlorides of uranium are prepared by reaction with additional chlorine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      All uranium chlorides react with water and air.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Bromide" title="Bromide">
       Bromides
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">
       iodides
      </a>
      of uranium are formed by direct reaction of, respectively,
      <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">
       bromine
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">
       iodine
      </a>
      with uranium or by adding
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UH
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      to those element's acids.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Known examples include:
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UBr
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      ,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UBr
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      ,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UI
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
      </span>
      , and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UI
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      . Uranium oxyhalides are water-soluble and include
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       F
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UOCl
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ,
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       Cl
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      , and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       Br
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      . Stability of the oxyhalides decrease as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">
       atomic weight
      </a>
      of the component halide increases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_85-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">
       Isotopes
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_concentrations">
       Natural concentrations
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_uranium" title="Isotopes of uranium">
       Isotopes of uranium
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_uranium" title="Natural uranium">
       Natural uranium
      </a>
      consists of three major
      <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">
       isotopes
      </a>
      :
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">
       uranium-238
      </a>
      (99.28%
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">
       natural abundance
      </a>
      ), uranium-235 (0.71%), and
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium-234" title="Uranium-234">
       uranium-234
      </a>
      (0.0054%). All three are
      <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">
       radioactive
      </a>
      , emitting
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">
       alpha particles
      </a>
      , with the exception that all three of these isotopes have small probabilities of undergoing
      <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">
       spontaneous fission
      </a>
      , rather than
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_emission" title="Alpha emission">
       alpha emission
      </a>
      . There are also five other trace isotopes: uranium-239, which is formed when
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U undergoes spontaneous fission, releasing neutrons that are captured by another
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U atom; uranium-237, which is formed when
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U captures a neutron but emits two more, which then decays to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neptunium-237" title="Neptunium-237">
       neptunium-237
      </a>
      ; and finally, uranium-233, which is formed in the
      <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">
       decay chain
      </a>
      of that neptunium-237. It is also expected that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium-232" title="Thorium-232">
       thorium-232
      </a>
      should be able to undergo
      <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">
       double beta decay
      </a>
      , which would produce uranium-232, but this has not yet been observed experimentally.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Uranium-238 is the most stable isotope of uranium, with a
      <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">
       half-life
      </a>
      of about 4.468
      <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
       ×
      </span>
      10
      <sup>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        9
       </span>
      </sup>
      years, roughly the
      <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">
       age of the Earth
      </a>
      . Uranium-235 has a half-life of about 7.13
      <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
       ×
      </span>
      10
      <sup>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        8
       </span>
      </sup>
      years, and uranium-234 has a half-life of about 2.48
      <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
       ×
      </span>
      10
      <sup>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        5
       </span>
      </sup>
      years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem777_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem777-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For natural uranium, about 49% of its alpha rays are emitted by each of
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U atom, and also 49% by
      <sup>
       234
      </sup>
      U (since the latter is formed from the former) and about 2.0% of them by the
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      U. When the Earth was young, probably about one-fifth of its uranium was uranium-235, but the percentage of
      <sup>
       234
      </sup>
      U was probably much lower than this.
     </p>
     <p>
      Uranium-238 is usually an α emitter (occasionally, it undergoes spontaneous fission), decaying through the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_series" title="Decay series">
       "Uranium Series" of nuclear decay
      </a>
      , which has 18 members, into
      <a href="/wiki/Lead#Isotopes" title="Lead">
       lead-206
      </a>
      , by a variety of different decay paths.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_series" title="Decay series">
       decay series of
       <sup>
        235
       </sup>
       U
      </a>
      , which is called the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_series" title="Decay series">
       actinium series
      </a>
      , has 15 members and eventually decays into lead-207.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The constant rates of decay in these decay series makes the comparison of the ratios of parent to daughter elements useful in
      <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">
       radiometric dating
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Uranium-234, which is a member of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_series" title="Decay series">
       "Uranium Series"
      </a>
      , decays to lead-206 through a series of relatively short-lived isotopes.
     </p>
     <p>
      Uranium-233 is made from
      <a href="/wiki/Thorium#Isotopes" title="Thorium">
       thorium-232
      </a>
      by neutron bombardment, usually in a nuclear reactor, and
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U is also fissile.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Its decay series ends at
      <a href="/wiki/Bismuth-209" title="Bismuth-209">
       bismuth-209
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">
       thallium
      </a>
      -205.
     </p>
     <p>
      Uranium-235 is important for both
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">
       nuclear reactors
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">
       nuclear weapons
      </a>
      , because it is the only uranium isotope existing in nature on Earth in any significant amount that is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissile" title="Fissile">
       fissile
      </a>
      . This means that it can be split into two or three fragments (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_products" title="Fission products">
       fission products
      </a>
      ) by thermal neutrons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Uranium-238 is not fissile, but is a fertile isotope, because after
      <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">
       neutron activation
      </a>
      it can produce
      <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">
       plutonium-239
      </a>
      , another fissile isotope. Indeed, the
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U nucleus can absorb one neutron to produce the radioactive isotope
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium-239" title="Uranium-239">
       uranium-239
      </a>
      .
      <sup>
       239
      </sup>
      U decays by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta_emission" title="Beta emission">
       beta emission
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">
       neptunium
      </a>
      -239, also a beta-emitter, that decays in its turn, within a few days into plutonium-239.
      <sup>
       239
      </sup>
      Pu was used as fissile material in the first
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" title="Atomic bomb">
       atomic bomb
      </a>
      detonated in the "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_test" title="Trinity test">
       Trinity test
      </a>
      " on 15 July 1945 in
      <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">
       New Mexico
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem773_31-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-EncyChem773-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Enrichment">
       Enrichment
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">
       Enriched uranium
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg">
        <img alt="A photo of a large hall filled with arrays of long white standing cylinders." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="3200" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg/220px-Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg/330px-Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg/440px-Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Cascades of
        <a href="/wiki/Gas_centrifuge" title="Gas centrifuge">
         gas centrifuges
        </a>
        are used to enrich uranium ore to concentrate its fissionable isotopes
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In nature, uranium is found as uranium-238 (99.2742%) and uranium-235 (0.7204%).
      <a href="/wiki/Isotope_separation" title="Isotope separation">
       Isotope separation
      </a>
      concentrates (enriches) the fissionable uranium-235 for nuclear weapons and most nuclear power plants, except for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_cooled_reactor" title="Gas cooled reactor">
       gas cooled reactors
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressurised_heavy_water_reactor" title="Pressurised heavy water reactor">
       pressurised heavy water reactors
      </a>
      . Most neutrons released by a fissioning atom of uranium-235 must impact other uranium-235 atoms to sustain the
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">
       nuclear chain reaction
      </a>
      . The concentration and amount of uranium-235 needed to achieve this is called a '
      <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">
       critical mass
      </a>
      '.
     </p>
     <p>
      To be considered 'enriched', the uranium-235 fraction should be between 3% and 5%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This process produces huge quantities of uranium that is depleted of uranium-235 and with a correspondingly increased fraction of uranium-238, called depleted uranium or 'DU'. To be considered 'depleted', the uranium-235 isotope concentration should be no more than 0.3%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paducah_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-paducah-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The price of uranium has risen since 2001, so enrichment tailings containing more than 0.35% uranium-235 are being considered for re-enrichment, driving the price of depleted uranium hexafluoride above $130 per kilogram in July 2007 from $5 in 2001.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paducah_90-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-paducah-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Gas_centrifuge" title="Gas centrifuge">
       gas centrifuge
      </a>
      process, where gaseous
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">
       uranium hexafluoride
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        6
       </span>
      </span>
      ) is separated by the difference in molecular weight between
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      UF
      <sub>
       6
      </sub>
      and
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      UF
      <sub>
       6
      </sub>
      using high-speed
      <a href="/wiki/Centrifuge" title="Centrifuge">
       centrifuges
      </a>
      , is the cheapest and leading enrichment process.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks478_30-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks478-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Gaseous_diffusion" title="Gaseous diffusion">
       gaseous diffusion
      </a>
      process had been the leading method for enrichment and was used in the
      <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">
       Manhattan Project
      </a>
      . In this process, uranium hexafluoride is repeatedly
      <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">
       diffused
      </a>
      through a
      <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">
       silver
      </a>
      -
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">
       zinc
      </a>
      membrane, and the different isotopes of uranium are separated by diffusion rate (since uranium 238 is heavier it diffuses slightly slower than uranium-235).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks478_30-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks478-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Molecular_laser_isotope_separation" title="Molecular laser isotope separation">
       molecular laser isotope separation
      </a>
      method employs a
      <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
       laser
      </a>
      beam of precise energy to sever the bond between uranium-235 and fluorine. This leaves uranium-238 bonded to fluorine and allows uranium-235 metal to precipitate from the solution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks479_6-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks479-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An alternative laser method of enrichment is known as
      <a href="/wiki/Atomic_vapor_laser_isotope_separation" title="Atomic vapor laser isotope separation">
       atomic vapor laser isotope separation
      </a>
      (AVLIS) and employs visible
      <a href="/wiki/Tunable_laser" title="Tunable laser">
       tunable lasers
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Dye_laser" title="Dye laser">
       dye lasers
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another method used is liquid thermal diffusion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciTechEncy_8-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-SciTechEncy-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Human_exposure">
       Human exposure
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      A person can be exposed to uranium (or its radioactive daughters, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">
       radon
      </a>
      ) by inhaling dust in air or by ingesting contaminated water and food. The amount of uranium in air is usually very small; however, people who work in factories that process
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">
       phosphate
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">
       fertilizers
      </a>
      , live near government facilities that made or tested nuclear weapons, live or work near a modern battlefield where depleted uranium
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weapons" title="Weapons">
       weapons
      </a>
      have been used, or live or work near a
      <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">
       coal
      </a>
      -fired power plant, facilities that mine or process uranium ore, or enrich uranium for reactor fuel, may have increased exposure to uranium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA-Rad_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EPA-Rad-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATSDR-ToxFAQ_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ATSDR-ToxFAQ-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Houses or structures that are over uranium deposits (either natural or man-made slag deposits) may have an increased incidence of exposure to radon gas. The
      <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">
       Occupational Safety and Health Administration
      </a>
      (OSHA) has set the
      <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">
       permissible exposure limit
      </a>
      for uranium exposure in the workplace as 0.25 mg/m
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      over an 8-hour workday. The
      <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">
       National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health
      </a>
      (NIOSH) has set a
      <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">
       recommended exposure limit
      </a>
      (REL) of 0.2 mg/m
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      over an 8-hour workday and a short-term limit of 0.6 mg/m
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      . At levels of 10 mg/m
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      , uranium is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">
       immediately dangerous to life and health
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most ingested uranium is excreted during
      <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">
       digestion
      </a>
      . Only 0.5% is absorbed when insoluble forms of uranium, such as its oxide, are ingested, whereas absorption of the more soluble
      <a href="/wiki/Uranyl" title="Uranyl">
       uranyl
      </a>
      ion can be up to 5%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, soluble uranium compounds tend to quickly pass through the body, whereas insoluble uranium compounds, especially when inhaled by way of dust into the
      <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">
       lungs
      </a>
      , pose a more serious exposure hazard. After entering the bloodstream, the absorbed uranium tends to
      <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">
       bioaccumulate
      </a>
      and stay for many years in
      <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">
       bone
      </a>
      tissue because of uranium's affinity for phosphates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Uranium is not absorbed through the skin, and
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">
       alpha particles
      </a>
      released by uranium cannot penetrate the skin.
     </p>
     <p>
      Incorporated uranium becomes
      <a href="/wiki/Uranyl" title="Uranyl">
       uranyl
      </a>
      ions, which accumulate in bone, liver, kidney, and reproductive tissues. Uranium can be decontaminated from steel surfaces
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francis_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Francis-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">
       aquifers
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wu-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_and_precautions">
       Effects and precautions
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Normal functioning of the
      <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">
       kidney
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">
       brain
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">
       liver
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">
       heart
      </a>
      , and other systems can be affected by uranium exposure, because, besides being weakly radioactive, uranium is a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic_metal" title="Toxic metal">
       toxic metal
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craft04_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Craft04-97">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATSDR_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ATSDR-98">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Uranium is also a reproductive toxicant.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hindin2005_99-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hindin2005-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Radiological effects are generally local because alpha radiation, the primary form of
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U decay, has a very short range, and will not penetrate skin.
      <a href="/wiki/Uranyl" title="Uranyl">
       Uranyl
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       UO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        2+
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ) ions, such as from
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_trioxide" title="Uranium trioxide">
       uranium trioxide
      </a>
      or uranyl nitrate and other hexavalent uranium compounds, have been shown to cause birth defects and immune system damage in laboratory animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the CDC has published one study that no human
      <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">
       cancer
      </a>
      has been seen as a result of exposure to natural or depleted uranium,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATSDR-PHS_102-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ATSDR-PHS-102">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
      exposure to uranium and its decay products, especially
      <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">
       radon
      </a>
      , are widely known and significant health threats.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Exposure to
      <a href="/wiki/Strontium-90" title="Strontium-90">
       strontium-90
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Iodine-131" title="Iodine-131">
       iodine-131
      </a>
      , and other fission products is unrelated to uranium exposure, but may result from medical procedures or exposure to spent reactor fuel or fallout from nuclear weapons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although accidental inhalation exposure to a high concentration of
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">
       uranium hexafluoride
      </a>
      has resulted in human fatalities, those deaths were associated with the generation of highly toxic hydrofluoric acid and
      <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_fluoride" title="Uranyl fluoride">
       uranyl fluoride
      </a>
      rather than with uranium itself.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Finely divided uranium metal presents a fire hazard because uranium is
      <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">
       pyrophoric
      </a>
      ; small grains will ignite spontaneously in air at room temperature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Uranium metal is commonly handled with gloves as a sufficient precaution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOH.WA_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DOH.WA-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Uranium concentrate is handled and contained so as to ensure that people do not inhale or ingest it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOH.WA_105-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DOH.WA-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable">
      <caption>
       Compilation of 2004 review on uranium toxicity
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craft04_97-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Craft04-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </caption>
      <tr>
       <th>
        Body system
       </th>
       <th>
        Human studies
       </th>
       <th>
        Animal studies
       </th>
       <th>
        <i>
         In vitro
        </i>
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Renal
       </td>
       <td>
        Elevated levels of protein excretion, urinary catalase and diuresis
       </td>
       <td>
        Damage to proximal convoluted tubules, necrotic cells cast from tubular epithelium, glomerular changes (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renal_failure" title="Renal failure">
         renal failure
        </a>
        )
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Brain/CNS
       </td>
       <td>
        Decreased performance on neurocognitive tests
       </td>
       <td>
        Acute cholinergic toxicity; Dose-dependent accumulation in cortex, midbrain, and vermis; Electrophysiological changes in hippocampus
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        DNA
       </td>
       <td>
        Increased reports of cancers
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ajph.org_48-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-ajph.org-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
         <a href="#cite_note-106">
          [106]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
         <a href="#cite_note-107">
          [107]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
         <a href="#cite_note-108">
          [108]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
         <a href="#cite_note-109">
          [109]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
         <a href="#cite_note-110">
          [110]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td>
        Increased mutagenicity (in mice) and induction of tumors
       </td>
       <td>
        Binucleated cells with micronuclei, Inhibition of cell cycle kinetics and proliferation; Sister chromatid induction, tumorigenic phenotype
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Bone/muscle
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
       <td>
        Inhibition of periodontal bone formation; and alveolar wound healing
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Reproductive
       </td>
       <td>
        Uranium miners have more first born female children
       </td>
       <td>
        Moderate to severe focal tubular atrophy; vacuolization of Leydig cells
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Lungs/respiratory
       </td>
       <td>
        No adverse health effects reported
       </td>
       <td>
        Severe nasal congestion and hemorrhage, lung lesions and fibrosis, edema and swelling, lung cancer
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Gastrointestinal
       </td>
       <td>
        Vomiting, diarrhea, albuminuria
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Liver
       </td>
       <td>
        No effects seen at exposure dose
       </td>
       <td>
        Fatty livers, focal necrosis
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Skin
       </td>
       <td>
        No exposure assessment data available
       </td>
       <td>
        Swollen vacuolated epidermal cells, damage to hair follicles and sebaceous glands
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Tissues surrounding embedded DU fragments
       </td>
       <td>
        Elevated uranium urine concentrations
       </td>
       <td>
        Elevated uranium urine concentrations, perturbations in biochemical and neuropsychological testing
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Immune system
       </td>
       <td>
        Chronic fatigue, rash, ear and eye infections, hair and weight loss, cough. May be due to combined chemical exposure rather than DU alone
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Eyes
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
       <td>
        Conjunctivitis, irritation inflammation, edema, ulceration of conjunctival sacs
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Blood
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
       <td>
        Decrease in RBC count and hemoglobin concentration
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Cardiovascular
       </td>
       <td>
        Myocarditis resulting from the uranium ingestion, which ended six months after ingestion
       </td>
       <td>
        No effects
       </td>
       <td>
        No studies
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
